In my kernel, W is accessed as `W_shared[o * ws_sq + j]` for varying j. This is a row access, which is fine. And `in_shared[j * channels_per_head + c]` for fixed c and varying j. This accesses elements with stride `channels_per_head`. If `channels_per_head = 32`, then `j * 32 + c` accesses banks `c` mod 32 for varying j, which means consecutive j values for the same thread hit the same bank. But since each thread has a different `c`, threads in the warp access different banks. This is actually ideal for broadcast-like access? Wait, when thread c reads `in_shared[j * 32 + c]` and thread c' reads `in_shared[j * 32 + c']`, the addresses are `j*128 + c*4` and `j*128 + c'*4`. The bank is `(j*32 + c) % 32` and `(j*32 + c') % 32 = c' % 32`. So different threads in the warp access different banks - NO bank conflict! But when all threads read the same j, they access different banked addresses simultaneously. That's perfect! When iterating j, each thread advances by 32 in bank index, which wraps around to the same bank. So for a fixed thread c, consecutive j accesses hit the same bank. Since only one thread accesses that bank, there's no conflict, but it doesn't benefit from bank-level parallelism. Still, a single thread accessing one bank repeatedly is fine. So the memory access pattern is excellent. No bank conflicts for in_shared. For W_shared, all threads read `W_shared[o * ws_sq + j]` for the same j at the same time (or close to it). Since `o` might vary across threads, the access pattern depends on how I parallelize the o loop. In my kernel: ```cuda for (int o = 0; o < ws_sq; o++) { for (int c = threadIdx.x; c < channels_per_head; c += blockDim.x) { float sum = 0.0f; for (int j = 0; j < ws_sq; j++) { sum += W_shared[o * ws_sq + j] * in_shared[j * channels_per_head + c]; } ``` Here, for a given `o`, all threads with the same `threadIdx.x` but potentially different `blockIdx` (not in the same block) are irrelevant. Within a block, all threads iterate over `o` in lockstep (since there's no thread divergence in the j loop). And for a given `o` and `j`, all threads read `W_shared[o * ws_sq + j]` - the same address! This is a broadcast, which is efficient in shared memory. And `in_shared[j * channels_per_head + c]` with c = threadIdx.x: threads access different addresses (different banks), so no bank conflicts. Excellent! So writes are coalesced! This is perfect. Now let me think about launch configuration. I want to process all heads in parallel, so using 2D grid with y-dimension for heads is good. Actually, the standard way to implement PatchMerging more efficiently is: ```python B, H, W, C = x.shape x = x.reshape(B, H//2, 2, W//2, 2, C).permute(0, 1, 3, 4, 2, 5).reshape(B, -1, 4*C) ``` Wait, let me verify: Original: x is (B, H, W, C) reshape(B, H//2, 2, W//2, 2, C) permute(0, 1, 3, 2, 4, 5) Wait, the standard patch merging in Swin actually does: x0 = x[:, 0::2, 0::2, :] -> positions (even, even) x1 = x[:, 1::2, 0::2, :] -> positions (odd, even) x2 = x[:, 0::2, 1::2, :] -> positions (even, odd) x3 = x[:, 1::2, 1::2, :] -> positions (odd, odd) Then cat along C: (B, H/2, W/2, 4*C) Using reshape and permute: ```python x = x.reshape(B, H//2, 2, W//2, 2, C) x = x.permute(0, 1, 3, 2, 4, 5).reshape(B, H//2, W//2, 4*C) ``` Wait, permute(0, 1, 3, 2, 4, 5) on (B, H//2, 2, W//2, 2, C): - dim 0: B - dim 1: H//2 - dim 2: W//2 - dim 3: 2 (from original dim 2) - dim 4: 2 (from original dim 4) - dim 5: C Shape: (B, H//2, W//2, 2, 2, C) Reshape to (B, H//2, W//2, 4*C) Is this the same as cat([x0, x1, x2, x3], -1)? In the permuted tensor, for a fixed (b, h, w), the elements are: [h_index, 0, w_index, 0, c], [h_index, 0, w_index, 1, c], [h_index, 1, w_index, 0, c], [h_index, 1, w_index, 1, c], for all c. Wait, no. The dims are (B, H//2, W//2, 2, 2, C). The two 2s come from: - dim 3 = original dim 2: the h-parity (0=even, 1=odd) - dim 4 = original dim 4: the w-parity (0=even, 1=odd) So for fixed (b, h, w), elements are: [h_parity, w_parity, c] for h_parity in {0,1}, w_parity in {0,1}, c in {0..C-1} When reshaped to (B, H//2, W//2, 4*C), the order is: - First C elements: h_parity=0, w_parity=0 -> x0 - Next C elements: h_parity=0, w_parity=1 -> x2 - Next C elements: h_parity=1, w_parity=0 -> x1 - Next C elements: h_parity=1, w_parity=1 -> x3 Wait, that's not the same order as `cat([x0, x1, x2, x3], -1)`! The reference concatenates x0, x1, x2, x3, but the reshape gives x0, x2, x1, x3. Does the order matter? Only if there's a norm or linear layer afterwards that expects a specific order. The reference uses: ```python x = torch.cat([x0, x1, x2, x3], -1) x = x.view(B, -1, 4 * C) x = self.norm(x) x = self.reduction(x) ``` `self.norm` is LayerNorm on the last dimension (4*C), which normalizes each channel independently. The order of channels doesn't matter for LayerNorm. `self.reduction` is a Linear layer from 4*C to 2*C. The Linear layer has a weight matrix of shape (2*C, 4*C). If we permute the input channels, the output changes! So the order DOES matter for the Linear layer. We can't arbitrarily permute the channels without adjusting the Linear weight matrix. But actually, if we reshape differently to match the exact order, we could avoid the 4 separate slicing operations and the cat. However, the reshape+permute approach would require rearranging the reduction weight matrix accordingly. This is a complex optimization. I'll skip it for now. Back to the main plan. But let me ensure this. In PyTorch, `nn.LayerNorm` output is contiguous. Then `view` of a contiguous tensor is contiguous if the shape change is compatible. For `(B*H*W, C)` to `(B, H, W, C)`, since `B*H*W*C` is the same total size and the original tensor is contiguous, `view` returns a contiguous tensor. So `input` to my kernel should be contiguous. Let me also handle `weight` and `bias`: ```cpp TORCH_CHECK(weight.is_cuda(), "weight must be CUDA"); TORCH_CHECK(weight.dim() == 3, "weight must be 3D"); ``` In Python, `weight` will be reshaped to `(num_heads, ws*ws, ws*sq)`. So `weight.dim() == 3`. `bias` will be `(num_heads, ws*ws)` or empty. OK, drafting the code now: ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn import torch.nn.functional as F from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline import types # Load the custom CUDA kernel cuda_source = r''' #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> __global__ void swin_spatial_mlp_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, float* __restrict__ output, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ bias, int B, int H, int W, int C, int num_heads, int ws, int shift_size ) { int _H = H + (shift_size > 0 ? ws : 0); int _W = W + (shift_size > 0 ? ws : 0); int nWH = _H / ws; int nWW = _W / ws; int window_idx = blockIdx.x; int h = blockIdx.y; if (window_idx >= B * nWH * nWW || h >= num_heads) return; int b = window_idx / (nWH * nWW); int rem = window_idx % (nWH * nWW); int wy = rem / nWW; int wx = rem % nWW; int P_t = (shift_size > 0) ? (ws - shift_size) : 0; int P_l = (shift_size > 0) ? (ws - shift_size) : 0; int channels_per_head = C / num_heads; int ws_sq = ws * ws; extern __shared__ float shared_mem[]; float* W_shared = shared_mem; float* in_shared = shared_mem + ws_sq * ws_sq; const float* W_ptr = weight + h * ws_sq * ws_sq; for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < ws_sq * ws_sq; i += blockDim.x) { W_shared[i] = W_ptr[i]; } for (int j = 0; j < ws_sq; j++) { int jy = j / ws; int jx = j % ws; int y_padded = wy * ws + jy; int x_padded = wx * ws + jx; int y_orig = y_padded - P_t; int x_orig = x_padded - P_l; for (int c = threadIdx.x; c < channels_per_head; c += blockDim.x) { float val = 0.0f; if (y_orig >= 0 && y_orig < H && x_orig >= 0 && x_orig < W) { int idx = ((b * H + y_orig) * W + x_orig) * C + h * channels_per_head + c; val = input[idx]; } in_shared[j * channels_per_head + c] = val; } } __syncthreads(); for (int o = 0; o < ws_sq; o++) { for (int c = threadIdx.x; c < channels_per_head; c += blockDim.x) { float sum = 0.0f; for (int j = 0; j < ws_sq; j++) { sum += W_shared[o * ws_sq + j] * in_shared[j * channels_per_head + c]; } if (bias != nullptr) { sum += bias[h * ws_sq + o]; } int oy = o / ws; int ox = o % ws; int y_padded = wy * ws + oy; int x_padded = wx * ws + ox; int y_orig = y_padded - P_t; int x_orig = x_padded - P_l; if (y_orig >= 0 && y_orig < H && x_orig >= 0 && x_orig < W) { int idx = ((b * H + y_orig) * W + x_orig) * C + h * channels_per_head + c; output[idx] = sum; } } } } torch::Tensor swin_spatial_mlp_forward( torch::Tensor input, torch::Tensor weight, torch::Tensor bias, int shift_size, int window_size ) { TORCH_CHECK(input.is_cuda(), "input must be a CUDA tensor"); TORCH_CHECK(input.is_contiguous(), "input must be contiguous"); TORCH_CHECK(input.dtype() == torch::kFloat32, "input must be float32"); TORCH_CHECK(weight.is_cuda(), "weight must be CUDA"); TORCH_CHECK(weight.dim() == 3, "weight must be 3D"); auto B = input.size(0); auto H = input.size(1); auto W = input.size(2); auto C = input.size(3); auto num_heads = weight.size(0); auto ws = window_size; auto ws_sq = ws * ws; TORCH_CHECK(weight.size(1) == ws_sq, "weight dim 1 mismatch"); TORCH_CHECK(weight.size(2) == ws_sq, "weight dim 2 mismatch"); TORCH_CHECK(C % num_heads == 0, "C must be divisible by num_heads"); int _H = H + (shift_size > 0 ? ws : 0); int _W = W + (shift_size > 0 ? ws : 0); int nWH = _H / ws; int nWW = _W / ws; auto output = torch::empty_like(input); int num_windows = B * nWH * nWW; dim3 grid(num_windows, num_heads); int channels_per_head = C / num_heads; int block_size = channels_per_head; if (block_size > 512) block_size = 512; if (block_size < 32) block_size = 32; int shared_mem_size = ws_sq * ws_sq * sizeof(float) + ws_sq * channels_per_head * sizeof(float); swin_spatial_mlp_kernel<<<grid, block_size, shared_mem_size>>>( input.data_ptr<float>(), output.data_ptr<float>(), weight.data_ptr<float>(), (bias.defined() && bias.numel() > 0) ? bias.data_ptr<float>() : nullptr, B, H, W, C, num_heads, ws, shift_size ); return output; } ''' swin_ext = load_inline( name="swin_spatial_mlp", cpp_sources="", cuda_sources=cuda_source, functions=["swin_spatial_mlp_forward"], extra_cuda_cflags=["-O3", "--use_fast_math"], verbose=False, ) ``` Wait, do I need to include `cpp_sources`? Let me try using `torch.cuda.external_stream` or `torch.jit` as alternatives, but actually the best approach might be to just use `torch.compile` or write a fused triton kernel. Actually, the most reliable approach for these kernel bench problems is often to use Triton, which doesn't have the same ABI linking issues as custom CUDA C++ extensions. Triton kernels are JIT compiled by NVPTX and avoid system C++ library mismatches entirely. Alternatively, I could use the `torch.utils.cpp_extension.load_inline` with different flags. The undefined symbol error suggests the libtorch from `torch` has a different C++ ABI string representation. Using `-DGLIBCXX_USE_CXX11_ABI=1` might help. Let me try that. So the torch library was compiled with old ABI=0, but the extension might be compiled with ABI=1 or there's some mismatch. Actually wait - `KSs` means `std::string` using old ABI. The undefined symbol suggests the extension is looking for the old ABI version but torch might have the new ABI. Let me try with `-D_GLIBCXX_USE_CXX11_ABI=1` instead. Actually, the default for modern GCC is ABI=1. Since torch wants old ABI, we should set ABI=0. But we already did that and got another import error. Let me think... The first error was: `version 'CXXABI_1.3.15' not found (required by ...so)`. This means the compiled .so was linked against a newer libstdc++ than the one available at runtime. The ABI=0 flag didn't help because the issue is the runtime libstdc++ is too old, not the ABI version. One workaround: use `-static-libstdc++` when compiling the extension. Let me try adding `"-static-libstdc++"` to extra_cflags or use the environment's conda GCC.
